reference PyTorch source (the prompt the agent sees)
import torch
import torch.nn as nn

class Model(nn.Module):
    """
    Performs a 3D transposed convolution operation with asymmetric input and square kernel.
    The input is padded before the convolution.

    Args:
        in_channels (int): Number of channels in the input tensor.
        out_channels (int): Number of channels produced by the convolution.
        kernel_size (int): Size of the square convolution kernel.
        stride (int, optional): Stride of the convolution. Defaults to 1.
        padding (int, optional): Padding applied to the input. Defaults to 0.
        groups (int, optional): Number of blocked connections from input channels to output channels. Defaults to 1.
        bias (bool, optional): If `True`, adds a learnable bias to the output. Defaults to `False`.
    """
    def __init__(self, in_channels: int, out_channels: int, kernel_size: int, stride: int = 1, padding: int = 0, output_padding: int = 0, groups: int = 1, bias: bool = False):
        super(Model, self).__init__()
        self.conv_transpose3d = nn.ConvTranspose3d(in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size=(kernel_size, kernel_size, kernel_size), stride=stride, padding=padding, groups=groups, bias=bias)

    def forward(self, x: torch.Tensor) -> torch.Tensor:
        """
        Performs the 3D transposed convolution.

        Args:
            x (torch.Tensor): Input tensor of shape (batch_size, in_channels, depth, height, width).

        Returns:
            torch.Tensor: Output tensor of shape (batch_size, out_channels, depth_out, height_out, width_out).
        """
        return self.conv_transpose3d(x)

# Test code
batch_size = 4
in_channels = 32
out_channels = 32
kernel_size = 3
depth = 32
height = 64
width = 128
stride = 2
padding = 1
groups = 4

def get_inputs():
    x = torch.rand(batch_size, in_channels, depth, height, width)
    return [x]

def get_init_inputs():
    return [in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride, padding, groups]

Let me analyze the problem: 1. We need to replace `nn.ConvTranspose3d` with a custom CUDA kernel. 2. The operation is a 3D transposed convolution with: - batch_size = 4 - in_channels = 32 - out_channels = 32 - kernel_size = 3 - depth = 32, height = 64, width = 128 - stride = 2 - padding = 1 - groups = 4 - output_padding = 0 (default) - bias = False (default) First, let me understand what ConvTranspose3d does. It's also called deconvolution or fractionally-strided convolution. For a transposed convolution with stride=2, padding=1, kernel_size=3: - The input dimensions are (D, H, W) - The output dimensions are: (D-1)*stride - 2*padding + kernel_size = (32-1)*2 - 2*1 + 3 = 62 - 2 + 3 = 63 - So output depth = height = width = 63 for a cubic input, but our input is 32x64x128 - Output depth = (32-1)*2 - 2*1 + 3 = 63 - Output height = (64-1)*2 - 2*1 + 3 = 125 - Output width = (128-1)*2 - 2*1 + 3 = 257 Wait, let me compute exactly: For transposed conv: out_size = (in_size - 1) * stride - 2 * padding + kernel_size + output_padding - depth_out = (32 - 1) * 2 - 2 * 1 + 3 + 0 = 62 - 2 + 3 = 63 - height_out = (64 - 1) * 2 - 2 * 1 + 3 + 0 = 126 - 2 + 3 = 127 - width_out = (128 - 1) * 2 - 2 * 1 + 3 + 0 = 254 - 2 + 3 = 255 Wait, let me check with PyTorch formula more carefully. For ConvTranspose3d, the output size is: L_out = (L_in - 1) * stride - 2 * padding + dilation * (kernel_size - 1) + output_padding + 1 Since dilation=1 by default: L_out = (L_in - 1) * stride - 2 * padding + kernel_size + output_padding So: - depth_out = (32-1)*2 - 2*1 + 3 + 0 = 62 - 2 + 3 = 63 - height_out = (64-1)*2 - 2*1 + 3 + 0 = 126 - 2 + 3 = 127 - width_out = (128-1)*2 - 2*1 + 3 + 0 = 254 - 2 + 3 = 255 Since groups=4, in_channels=32, out_channels=32: - Each group has 32/4 = 8 input channels and 32/4 = 8 output channels. For a transposed convolution, it's equivalent to convolving a dilated input with the transposed weight. More specifically, ConvTranspose3d is equivalent to: 1. Inserting `stride-1` zeros between input elements in each spatial dimension 2. Padding the dilated input with `kernel_size - padding - 1` on each side 3. Performing a regular convolution with the weight (but transposed in the channel dimensions) Actually, the implementation detail: in PyTorch, ConvTranspose doesn't actually transpose the weight tensor at runtime - the weight is stored directly for the transposed operation. But mathematically, it's equivalent to convolving the dilated input with the weight flipped in spatial dimensions, but that's getting complicated. For our custom kernel, we have a few options: 1. Use CUTLASS or cuDNN directly - but we likely can't import those easily 2. Implement a custom kernel that performs the transposed convolution directly Given the parameters (groups=4, batch=4, in_ch=32, out_ch=32), we could: - Unroll the transposed convolution as a direct computation - Implement a kernel where each thread computes one output element Actually, for grouped transposed conv with stride=2, one efficient approach is: - Transposed conv with stride 2 can be implemented as "scatter and accumulate" operations - Or equivalently: for each input element, multiply by the kernel and add to the appropriate region of the output For stride-2 transposed conv: - Each input element at (d, h, w) contributes to a k×k×k region in the output centered at (d*stride, h*stride, w*stride) or nearby. Actually, thinking about it carefully: ConvTranspose3d with stride=2 is equivalent to convolving a dilated input (where we insert zeros) with the kernel. Another way to see it: the gradient of Conv3d is ConvTranspose3d. For a direct implementation of ConvTranspose3d: For each output position (d, h, w), it's computed as: out[b, c_out, d, h, w] = sum_{c_in, kd, kh, kw} weight[c_in, c_out, kd, kh, kw] * input[b, c_in, d', h', w'] where d' = (d + padding - kd) / stride (must be integer), etc. So we need: d = d' * stride - padding + kd, which means d' = (d + padding - kd) / stride. Since stride=2, not every output position receives contributions from every kernel position. Or equivalently, for each input element, we can loop over the kernel and compute which output elements it contributes to: out[b, c_out, d'*stride - padding + kd, h'*stride - padding + kh, w'*stride - padding + kw] += input[b, c_in, d', h', w'] * weight[c_in, c_out, kd, kh, kw] This "scatter" approach is often more efficient for transposed conv with stride > 1, because each input element contributes to exactly k^3 output elements (when valid), and we can structure the kernel to have coalesced writes. Wait, actually for transposed conv, the standard efficient implementation when stride > 1 IS the scatter approach: each input pixel is multiplied by the kernel and scattered to multiple output locations. With groups=4, in_ch=32, out_ch=32: - group_size_in = 8 - group_size_out = 8 - batch = 4 - So we have 4 * 4 = 16 groups total (batch * groups) For a scatter approach kernel: ```cuda __global__ void conv_transpose3d_scatter( float* out, const float* in, const float* weight, int batch, int in_channels, int out_channels, int depth_in, int height_in, int width_in, int depth_out, int height_out, int width_out, int kernel_size, int stride, int padding, int groups) { int g = blockIdx.x; // group int b = blockIdx.y; // batch int total_in_ch = in_channels / groups; int total_out_ch = out_channels / groups; // For each input position, thread computes all out_ch contributions int in_ch = blockIdx.z; // which input channel within group int spatial_idx = threadIdx.x + blockDim.x * blockIdx.z? } ``` Actually, we need to be more careful. Let's think about the launch configuration. With the scatter approach, for each input element, each of the 8 output channels in the group gets contributions from 3*3*3=27 kernel elements. So each input element generates 8*27=216 multiply-adds. Total input elements: batch * 32 * 32 * 64 * 128 = 4 * 32 * 262144 = 33,554,432 elements. Each generates 216 ops = ~7.2 billion ops. At ~20 TFLOPS that's 0.35 ms, plus memory bandwidth. Memory bandwidth for reading input: 33.5M * 4 bytes = 134 MB Memory bandwidth for reading weight: 32 * 32 * 27 * 4 bytes = ~110 KB (tiny, cached) Memory bandwidth for writing output: 4 * 32 * 63 * 127 * 255 * 4 bytes = ~1 GB Output size calculation: depth_out = 63, height_out = 127, width_out = 255 Total output elements: 4 * 32 * 63 * 127 * 255 ≈ 4 * 32 * 2,040,555 ≈ 261,191,040 elements Output memory: ~1.04 GB If we do atomic adds for scatter, we might have contention. But since each input element touches a distinct set of output elements, and if we assign each input element to one thread (or warp), we can have each thread accumulate into local registers and then write without atomics, OR we can have different threads write to overlapping output regions and need atomics. Wait, actually if two different input elements map to overlapping output regions, we need to accumulate. This happens because the kernel size is 3 and stride is 2. Yes, adjacent input elements with stride=2 and kernel=3 produce overlapping outputs: - Input at position i produces output centered at roughly 2i - The footprint has radius 1, so input i affects outputs [2i-1, 2i+1] - Input i+1 affects [2i+1, 2i+3] - They overlap at 2i+1! So if we use the scatter approach, we DO need atomic operations when multiple input elements write to the same output location. This is actually why transposed conv is tricky. An alternative is the "gather" approach, where each output element gathers from input elements: For output at (d, h, w), we find all input positions (d', h', w') such that: d = d' * stride - padding + kd for some kd in [0, kernel_size-1] So d' = (d + padding - kd) / stride, which must be integer and in range. This means we launch one thread per output element. Each thread computes: sum over valid (kd, kh, kw, c_in, group) of weight * input For stride=2, kernel=3, padding=1: For a given output d, there may be at most ceil(3/2) = 2 valid input positions? Actually: d' = (d + 1 - kd) / 2, for kd in {0,1,2} For a fixed d, only some kd give integer d': - If d is even: kd=1 gives (even+1-1)/2 = even/2 = integer. kd=... let's check: d=0: kd=1 -> 0, kd=... Actually: d' = (d + 1 - kd)/2. For d=0: kd=1 -> 0; kd=... (0+1-0)/2 = 0.5 no; (0+1-2)/2 = -0.5 no. So only kd=1 works for d=0. Wait, that doesn't seem right. Let me check. Actually, the formula for transposed conv is: output = conv_transpose(input, weight) For a standard conv with stride=2, kernel=3, padding=1: out_conv = floor((in + 2*padding - kernel)/stride) + 1 For in=63: floor((63+2-3)/2)+1 = floor(62/2)+1 = 31+1=32. Good. The transposed operation maps: out[b, c_out, d, h, w] = sum_{kd, kh, kw, c_in} weight[c_in, c_out, kd, kh, kw] * input[b, c_in, d', h', w'] where: d' = (d + padding - kd) / stride when (d + padding - kd) % stride == 0 and d' in [0, depth_in) Wait, I think I've been using the wrong formula. In PyTorch ConvTranspose, the weight shape is (in_channels, out_channels/groups, kD, kH, kW). Actually, looking at PyTorch docs: - weight: (in_channels, out_channels/groups, kD, kH, kW) The operation computes: out(N_i, C_out_j) = bias(C_out_j) + sum_k sum_z sum_y sum_x weight(C_in_k, C_out_j, z, y, x) * input(N_i, C_in_k, stride_d * d + z - padding, ...) Hmm, let me look up exactly how PyTorch computes ConvTranspose3d. From PyTorch source / cuDNN: ConvTranspose is equivalent to: 1. Dilate the input by inserting stride-1 zeros between elements 2. Pad with kernel_size - padding - 1 on each side 3. Do a regular convolution But for the direct mathematical formula, the gradient of conv w.r.t input is conv_transpose. If forward conv is: y = conv(x, w) with stride S, padding P Then backward w.r.t x is basically conv_transpose(grad_y, w) For each output position (d, h, w) of the CONV_TRANSPOSE: it receives contributions from input at positions where: (dilated_input position) = (d, h, w) convolved with flipped kernel Actually, a more practical formula. If we think of conv_transpose as upsampling followed by conv: 1. Create dilated_input of size (D_in-1)*stride + 1 - dilated_input[..., i*stride, ...] = input[..., i, ...] 2. Pad dilated_input with padding_dilated = kernel_size - padding - 1 on each side 3. Do regular conv with stride=1 on dilated_input, same weight Wait, but the weight in conv_transpose is stored as (in_ch, out_ch/groups, kD, kH, kW), while regular conv weight is (out_ch, in_ch/groups, kD, kH, kW). Actually they're transposed in channel dimensions. For our custom kernel, the simplest and most correct approach is: Option A: Implement the direct definition as a gather kernel (each thread computes one output element) Option B: Use im2col + GEMM approach Option C: Use a CUTLASS/cuDNN wrapper Given that this is a specific configuration (groups=4, stride=2, kernel=3), a direct custom kernel might be competitive if well-tuned. But before writing a complex kernel, let me think about whether we can just call an existing well-optimized primitive. CuDNN supports conv transpose, but PyTorch already uses it. The reference is nn.ConvTranspose3d which uses cuDNN (or fallback). Beating cuDNN on a standard conv is hard. However, maybe the reference doesn't use cuDNN optimally, or maybe for this specific small size, a custom kernel could win. But more likely, we need to be smart. Wait! For group conv with group size 8 (in_ch=32, groups=4), we have 4 groups of 8->8 channels. This is essentially 4 independent conv transposes. Can we decompose the transposed convolution? The task is to write a custom CUDA kernel for 3D transposed convolution. Let's analyze the reference implementation: ```python nn.ConvTranspose3d(in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size=(kernel_size, kernel_size, kernel_size), stride=stride, padding=padding, groups=groups, bias=bias) ``` with parameters: - batch_size = 4 - in_channels = 32 - out_channels = 32 - kernel_size = 3 - depth = 32, height = 64, width = 128 - stride = 2 - padding = 1 - groups = 4 First, let me understand the shape math for ConvTranspose3d: - Input: (N, C_in, D_in, H_in, W_in) - Output: (N, C_out, D_out, H_out, W_out) where - D_out = (D_in - 1) * stride - 2 * padding + kernel_size + output_padding - With output_padding = 0: - D_out = (32 - 1) * 2 - 2 * 1 + 3 = 31 * 2 - 2 + 3 = 62 - 2 + 3 = 63 - H_out = (64 - 1) * 2 - 2 * 1 + 3 = 63 * 2 - 2 + 3 = 126 - 2 + 3 = 127 - W_out = (128 - 1) * 2 - 2 * 1 + 3 = 127 * 2 - 2 + 3 = 254 - 2 + 3 = 255 Wait, let me verify that: (128-1)*2 = 127*2 = 254, 254 - 2 + 3 = 255 Hmm, the standard formula is: D_out = (D_in - 1) * stride - 2*padding + dilation*(kernel_size - 1) + output_padding + 1 Wait no, for ConvTranspose3d, if input was generated by Conv3d with the same parameters, the formula depends on how PyTorch defines it. Actually for PyTorch ConvTranspose3d: D_out = (D_in - 1) * stride - 2 * padding + dilation * (kernel_size - 1) + output_padding + 1 With dilation = 1 (default): D_out = (D_in - 1) * stride - 2 * padding + (kernel_size - 1) + output_padding + 1 D_out = (32 - 1) * 2 - 2 * 1 + 2 + 0 + 1 = 31*2 - 2 + 3 = 62 - 2 + 3 = 63 Wait that's 62 - 2 + 3 = 63. But why kernel_size = 3 gives kernel_size - 1 = 2, plus 1 = 3. So it's -2 + 3 = +1. Let's recalculate: 31*2 = 62, 62 - 2 + 3 = 63. That matches my previous. Actually many resources define it as: D_out = (D_in - 1) * stride - 2*padding + kernel_size + output_padding Is that the same? With kernel_size=3: 62 - 2 + 3 + 0 = 63. Yes! Because (kernel_size - 1) + 1 = kernel_size. OK so output is (4, 32, 63, 127, 255). groups = 4 means we have 4 groups: in_channels/groups = 32/4 = 8 channels per group, out_channels/groups = 32/4 = 8 channels per group. So it's group-wise transposed convolution. Transposed convolution can be thought of as: 1. Upsample by inserting zeros between input elements according to stride 2. Apply standard convolution on the upsampled tensor Or equivalently, it can be computed as direct convolution where each kernel element is multiplied by the corresponding input and accumulated to appropriate output locations. For efficient CUDA kernel, I can think of a few approaches: 1. Use cuDNN via torch (but that's what the reference does, so we need to beat it) 2. Write a custom kernel that leverages the specific problem size The key insight for transposed convolution is that it can be implemented as a "reverse" convolution where input elements contribute to output patches. Since the stride is 2, each input element generates a kernel_size^3 = 27 output elements (for each output channel in the group). For a group transposed conv, the computation is: For each output position (d, h, w), output channel c_out: out[n, c_out, d, h, w] = sum over c_in in the same group, and over kernel offsets (kd, kh, kw) such that this input position maps to this output position: weight[c_out, c_in, kd, kh, kw] * input[n, c_in, id, ih, iw] Where the mapping is: id = floor((d + padding - kd) / stride) when (d + padding - kd) % stride == 0 Wait, transposed conv is more naturally viewed as: For each input position (id, ih, iw), output base position is: d_start = id * stride - padding And then kernel offsets add to that. Actually for ConvTranspose3d (without output_padding): The output location from input (id, ih, iw) and kernel offset (kd, kh, kw) is: d = id * stride + kd - padding h = ih * stride + kh - padding w = iw * stride + kw - padding Wait, I need to verify. For standard conv with stride S, padding P: d_out = floor((d_in + 2P - K) / S) + 1 For conv transpose to reconstruct input, if output is (D_out, H_out, W_out): Actually, PyTorch computes conv_transpose as the gradient of conv. For a transposed conv with stride S, padding P: If input is id, then the corresponding output starts at id * S. The kernel is placed starting at that position, but shifted by P. So the actual output positions for kernel element (kd, kh, kw) from input (id, ih, iw) is: d = id * S - P + kd h = ih * S - P + kh w = iw * S - P + kw Yes, that's correct for conv_transpose. So each input pixel creates a K×K×K patch in the output, centered/offset by the padding. For our case: - S = 2 - P = 1 - K = 3 - Input: (4, 32, 32, 64, 128) - Output: (4, 32, 63, 127, 255) For id=0: output positions are d = 0*2 - 1 + kd = -1, 0, 1. So d=0 corresponds to kd=1. For id=1: output positions are d = 2 - 1 + kd = 1, 2, 3. So d=1: kd=0, d=2: kd=1, d=3: kd=2. For id=31: output positions are d = 62 - 1 + kd = 61, 62, 63. But output is only up to 62 (63 elements: 0-62). So d=61: kd=0, d=62: kd=1, d=63 is out of bounds (kd=2 is clipped). Let me verify the output size formula again: D_out = (32 - 1) * 2 - 2*1 + 3 = 62 - 2 + 3 = 63. Yes, indices 0 to 62. For id=31: d = 61, 62, 63. So kd=2 (d=63) is outside. It doesn't contribute. Wait, but PyTorch handles this by the formula, so indeed we clip to output boundaries. Actually for standard conv_transpose in PyTorch, the kernel elements that would map outside are simply not summed. But how does this match the "equivalent conv" view? Hmm, let's think differently. For efficiency on GPU, there are a few approaches: **Approach A: Use im2col + GEMM** ConvTranspose3d can be done by: 1. Rearranging input into columns (this essentially does the dilation/stride part) 2. Multiply with weight matrix 3. Add bias 4. Reshape/accumulate into output But this requires significant temporary memory and may not be faster than cuDNN's highly optimized implementation for 3D conv. However, for our specific small sizes, a custom kernel might beat cuDNN's general overhead. **Approach B: Direct element computation** Each thread computes one output element. For output (n, c_out, d, h, w), we sum over: - c_in in group (8 channels) - kd where (d - kd + padding) % stride == 0 and the corresponding id is in range - kh, kw similarly Wait, no. The natural direct formula for output element is: out[n, c_out, d, h, w] = sum_{c_in} sum_{kd, kh, kw where d = id*stride - padding + kd for some valid id} weight[c_out, c_in, kd, kh, kw] * in[n, c_in, id, ih, iw] For each output position (d, h, w), there are at most floor(K/stride)+1 possible input positions that contribute to it in each dimension. K = 3, stride = 2. For d=0: id could be... d = id*2 - 1 + kd. If kd=1, id=0. Only 1 valid combination. If kd=0, id=0.5 (no). kd=2, id=-0.5 (no). So only 1. For d=1: kd=0, id=1 (1=1*2-1+0=1). kd=2, id=0 (1=0*2-1+2=1). So 2 valid combinations. For d=2: kd=1, id=1 (2=1*2-1+1=2). kd=... no other. So 1. For d=3: kd=0, id=2; kd=2, id=1. So 2. So in each spatial dimension, at most 2 input positions map to each output position. Since K=3, S=2, there are ceil(K/S) = 2 "phases" or overlapping regions. The total number of contributing (kd, kh, kw) combinations per output element is at most 2*2*2 = 8. And for each of those, c_in from 0 to 7 (8 channels). So at most 8 * 8 = 64 multiplications per output element. With 4 groups of 8 channels each, and 32 output channels: Total output elements = 4 * 32 * 63 * 127 * 255 ≈ 4 * 32 * 2,040,255 ≈ 261,152,640 Each output needs about 64 FMAs in the worst case. Total FLOPs ≈ 33.4 billion FMAs ≈ 67 GFLOPs. That's small enough that memory bandwidth might matter, but H100 is very fast. Wait, let me recount. For each output element: - Number of c_in = 8 - Number of (kd, kh, kw) that map: varies. On average, for interior it's 8 combinations, for edges/boundaries less. Actually let's think: for 3D, each output point is computed from on average (roughly) 8 * 8 = 64 products. Total output elements: 4 * 32 * 63 * 127 * 255 = 261,152,640 Total MACs: 261,152,640 * 64 ≈ 16.7 billion. In FLOPs (multiply + add): ~33.5 GFLOPs. At 67 TFLOPS FP32, this is only 0.05% of peak compute. At 3.35 TB/s memory bandwidth, if we need to read input (4*32*32*64*128 = 33,554,432 floats = 134MB), weight (32*8*3*3*3 = 6,912 floats = 27KB), and write output (261M floats = 1GB). So the mapping is correct. The dilated input has non-zeros at even positions {0, 2, 4, ..., 62}. Now for 3D: non-zero at (even, even, even) positions in the dilated tensor. This is very structured! The regular conv on this tensor only ever sees non-zero inputs at (2*id, 2*ih, 2*iw). For output (d, h, w), the conv accesses dilated input at: u[d-1+k_d, h-1+k_h, w-1+k_w] for k_d, k_h, k_w in {0,1,2}. This is non-zero ONLY when (d-1+k_d), (h-1+k_h), (w-1+k_w) are all even. Which means: - d-1+k_d ≡ 0 (mod 2) - h-1+k_h ≡ 0 (mod 2) - w-1+k_w ≡ 0 (mod 2) Since k_d, k_h, k_w ∈ {0,1,2}, and we need exactly the even positions: If d is even: d-1 is odd, so k_d must be odd (i.e., k_d=1). If d is odd: d-1 is even, so k_d must be even (k_d ∈ {0, 2}). This is exactly what we found earlier: even output positions require k=1 (center kernel), odd positions allow k=0 or k=2. And when k_d=1, the input index is id = (d-1+1)/2 = d/2. When k_d=0, id = (d-1+0)/2 = (d-1)/2. When k_d=2, id = (d-1+2)/2 = (d+1)/2. So we have a very clear phase structure. **Algorithm: Phase-based computation** We can split the output into 8 phases based on (d%2, h%2, w%2): 1. (0,0,0): needs k_d=1, k_h=1, k_w=1 → 1 kernel position per c_in 2. (0,0,1): k_d=1, k_h=1, k_w∈{0,2} → 2 positions 3. (0,1,0): k_d=1, k_h∈{0,2}, k_w=1 → 2 positions 4. (0,1,1): k_d=1, k_h∈{0,2}, k_w∈{0,2} → 4 positions 5. (1,0,0): k_d∈{0,2}, k_h=1, k_w=1 → 2 positions 6. (1,0,1): k_d∈{0,2}, k_h=1, k_w∈{0,2} → 4 positions 7. (1,1,0): k_d∈{0,2}, k_h∈{0,2}, k_w=1 → 4 positions 8. (1,1,1): k_d∈{0,2}, k_h∈{0,2}, k_w∈{0,2} → 8 positions For phase (0,0,0), each output position only needs 1*8=8 MACs (just one spatial position, across 8 c_in channels). For phase (0,0,1): 2*8=16 MACs. For phase (1,1,1): 8*8=64 MACs. Actually, we can write a direct kernel that handles each phase efficiently. But for a unified kernel, the phase information is implicit in the loop bounds. **Simplified Direct Kernel:** What if we just write a straightforward fused kernel where: - Each thread computes one output element. - The thread loops over c_in=0..7. - For each c_in, it figures out which kernel positions apply based on (d%2, h%2, w%2). With 261M output elements, and each thread doing ~27 FMAs, the arithmetic intensity is very low. The bottleneck will be reading weights and input. But with good caching (L2), maybe weights will be cached since all threads in a block read the same weights? Actually, no: threads computing different c_out read different weight slices. For output (n, c_out, d, h, w), weights needed are weight[c_out, :, :, :, :] (8*27 floats). If threads are coarsened over c_out, then within a warp different threads read different weights → no broadcast benefit. If threads parallelize over spatial with same c_out, they all read the same weights → broadcast from L1/L2! That's great. So if we process one c_out at a time (or a slice of c_out), and launch many threads to parallelize spatial dimensions, the weights are shared across all threads. Similarly, input is shared across all c_out for the same spatial position. **Optimal thread organization:** For each fixed (n, c_out), compute all (d, h, w). - # positions = 63 * 127 * 255 ≈ 2.04M. - With 2.04M threads just for one c_out, that's already plenty of parallelism. - Threads in same block (computing same c_out, spatial neighbors) share weights and input. - Total c_out = 32. We can have 32 separate "task streams" per batch. But 2.04M threads for one layer might cause some overhead. On H100, each SM can run up to 2048 threads, and with 132 SMs, max concurrent threads is ~270K. So 2M threads is fine (7 waves). Actually, we can organize: - Each block computes a spatial tile for one (n, c_out). - Thread block size: 256 or 512. - Grid: (n, c_out, spatial_tiles). For spatial tile, say 4*8*16 = 512 positions. - Threads handle the tile, each computing 2 or fewer positions if 256 threads. - Actually, 256 threads for 512 positions = 2 positions per thread. Input tile for (4,8,16) output: roughly (3,5,9) input positions. With 8 c_in: 3*5*9*8 = 1080 floats = 4.3KB. Weights: 8*27 = 216 floats = 864 bytes. Shared memory total: ~5KB per block. Very small! We could use even larger tiles. For example, output tile 8*16*32 = 4096. Input tile: 5*9*17 = 765 positions * 8 c_in = 6120 floats = 24.5KB. Weights: 216 floats = 864 bytes. Total: 25.4KB. Still under 48KB shared memory. With 4096 output positions and 256 threads: 16 positions per thread. Or with 512 threads: 8 positions per thread. This is actually very efficiency-friendly. Let me think about memory access patterns when loading input into shared memory. Input tensor shape: (N, C, D, H, W) = (4, 32, 32, 64, 128). We need to load a sub-tensor: (n, c_in_group, id:id+ID, ih:ih+IH, iw:iw+IW). Since c_in ranges over 8 channels, we load a 8*ID*IH*IW chunk. With 256 threads, each thread loads (8*ID*IH*IW)/256 floats. For ID=5, IH=9, IW=17: 8*5*9*17 = 6120. 6120/256 ≈ 24 floats per thread. That's fine. Then after loading input and weights to shared memory, each thread computes its output elements by reading from shared memory. The computation for one thread: For each assigned output position (d, h, w): sum = 0 For c_in in 0..7: For each valid (kd, kh, kw) based on parity: id = (d + 1 - kd) / 2 ih = (h + 1 - kh) / 2 iw = (w + 1 - kw) / 2 sum += weight[c_out, c_in, kd, kh, kw] * input[n, c_in, id, ih, iw] output[n, c_out, d, h, w] = sum Since shared memory has the relevant data, these accesses are very fast. Wait, can we even load weights into registers directly? 8*27=216 floats per c_out. For each block handling one c_out, all threads need the full weight slice of 216 floats. That's 864 bytes. We can load this into shared memory once per block (broadcast). Actually, an even better approach: since all threads in a warp/block computing the same c_out read the same weights, if weights are in shared memory, there's no bank conflict (broadcast). **Shared memory layout:** For input: store as [c_in][id][ih][iw] or [id][ih][iw][c_in] for coalesced load. For weights: store as [c_in][kd][kh][kw]. Actually, when computing, a thread will need weight[c_in][kd][kh][kw] and input[c_in][id][ih][iw]. If we loop c_in outermost, we can read weight[c_in,:,:,:] once (or use a register for the whole c_in if 27 floats fit) and reuse it for all spatial positions a thread computes. And similarly for input. Actually, we can have each thread compute multiple output positions by unrolling. But the key is that weight for one c_in is 27 floats. At most 8 of them are used per output position (based on parity). So if a thread computes multiple positions with different parities, it might need different subsets. To maximize register reuse, a thread could compute all its assigned positions for ONE c_in before moving to next c_in. **Addressing partial sums:** If a block computes output for c_out over a spatial tile, and we iterate c_in from 0 to 7: - Load weight[c_out, c_in, :, :, :] into registers (27 floats). Actually 27*4=108 bytes. This fits in registers. - For each thread, loop over its output positions, read from input shared memory, accumulate. - Move to next c_in. This is excellent because weight is loaded only 8 times per block (once per c_in), and each load benefits all threads. Wait, but loading weight for all threads: if weight is in shared memory, each thread can read it. The 27 floats per c_in per block means 27*4=108 bytes of shared memory for weights. Total for all c_in if loaded sequentially: just 108 bytes at a time in shared memory, or just each thread loads weight into registers from global memory? Actually, global memory loads for weights would benefit from L2 cache since all blocks computing the same c_out read the same weights. And within a block, L1 cache can help. Actually, weights are only 32*8*27 = 6912 floats = 27KB total. They easily fit in L2 cache. So even reading from global memory without shared memory might be OK if threads in a warp read the same weights simultaneously (broadcast from L1/L2). For inputs, spatial tiles don't overlap much between adjacent output tiles because stride=2. But within a block, threads do share input. So shared memory for input is important. **Proposed Kernel:** ```cuda template<int TD, int TH, int TW> __global__ void conv_transpose3d_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, float* __restrict__ output, int N, int C_in, int C_out, int D_in, int H_in, int W_in, int D_out, int H_out, int W_out, int K, int S, int P, int groups) { // Each block: (n, c_out, tile_idx) int n = blockIdx.x; // or block mapping int c_out = blockIdx.y; int tile_idx = blockIdx.z; int c_per_group = C_in / groups; int g = c_out / c_per_group; // c_out's group int c_out_local = c_out % c_per_group; // Convert tile_idx to spatial tile origin int num_tiles_d = (D_out + TD - 1) / TD; int num_tiles_h = (H_out + TH - 1) / TH; int num_tiles_w = (W_out + TW - 1) / TW; int tile_d = tile_idx / (num_tiles_h * num_tiles_w); int tile_h = (tile_idx / num_tiles_w) % num_tiles_h; int tile_w = tile_idx % num_tiles_w; int d0 = tile_d * TD; int h0 = tile_h * TH; int w0 = tile_w * TW; // Load input tile into shared memory __shared__ float s_input[TILE_C_IN * TILE_D * TILE_H * TILE_W]; __shared__ float s_weight[8 * 27]; // max c_in * kernel_size // ... } ``` This is getting complicated to write generically. Let me think if there's a simpler approach that still performs well. **Alternative: Use PyTorch's unfold / matrix multiplication trick but with custom kernel** Actually, there's an even simpler idea. What if we manually implement conv_transpose as a strided conv after explicit dilation? For example: ```python # Equivalent to transposed conv with K=3, S=2, P=1 # 1. Dilate input: insert zeros # 2. Conv3d with stride=1, padding=1 ``` The issue is that materializing the dilated tensor costs memory bandwidth: 134MB read, but writes ~500MB of zeros? Actually dilated size is 4*32*63*127*255*4 bytes ≈ 1GB, with 1/8 non-zero. Writing this and reading it back in conv is expensive. But maybe we can use a custom kernel for step 1 (dilation + conv combined), which is what I was thinking. **Even simpler: Use CUTLASS-like implicit GEMM but as a direct kernel** Actually, let's look at what might beat cuDNN. For grouped 3D conv transpose, cuDNN might use an algorithm that's not perfectly tuned for this small group/channel size. A well-written custom kernel could win simply by avoiding cuDNN's overhead and exactly matching the access pattern. Let me write a relatively simple custom kernel: - One thread per output element. - Register accumulation. - Explicitly unrolled inner loop based on parity. Since weights fit in L2 well and the kernel is small, and input reads might be cached, a direct kernel could work. Plus, output coalescing is perfect. Actually wait, output elements: 261M floats. At 4 bytes each, writing output is 1GB. That's the dominant memory cost. No matter what compute we do, we must write 1GB. If we write output once with one thread per element, and reads are cached, the kernel might be bounded by ~1GB/s of writes plus some reads. At 3TB/s, that's ~0.3ms. PyTorch/cuDNN likely also reaches near this bound. To beat it, we need better compute efficiency and memory coalescing/latency hiding. Actually, one thing we can do is avoid atomic operations. cuDNN's transpose conv implementation might use some implicit GEMM that has workspace overhead or algorithm selection overhead. For small batch/group configurations, direct kernels can sometimes win. Let me look at the problem from another angle: the shape (4, 32, 63, 127, 255) is pretty specific. cuDNN's 3D conv is sometimes slower than expected. **Middle-ground approach: Direct kernel with good smem for input, registers for weights** I'll write a kernel where: - BlockDim = 256 threads. - Grid covers all (n, c_out) pairs plus spatial tiles. - For each (n, c_out) block, compute a tile of (TD=4, TH=4, TW=16) spatial positions. - Thread output count = 4*4*16 = 256. With 256 threads, 1 output per thread. - Shared memory loads the required input for all 8 c_in channels. - Input tile: approx (3, 5, 9) * 8 = ~1080 floats. - Weight slice for c_out is loaded to shared memory or fetched cooperatively. - Weight: 8 * 27 = 216 floats. - Shared memory total: ~5KB. So per block: - 256 threads - 256 outputs (1 per thread) - ~5KB shared memory - 8 outer loop iterations over c_in - Each c_in iteration: threads read weight[8*27 floats] from smem, and input[3*5*9*8 floats] from smem. Wait, all threads read the same weight for each c_in, but different input values. So weight can be broadcast from smem, input needs different addresses per thread. Actually, let me structure it so that each thread handles a specific (d, h, w) and fixed c_out. For a given (d, h, w), which inputs are needed? id = (d+1-kd)/2 for valid kd. Only 1 or 2 values of kd are valid per dimension, so at most 8 combinations. And the corresponding id values are adjacent or near-adjacent. For example, d=5 (odd, d%2=1): kd=0: id=(5+1)/2=3 kd=2: id=(5-1)/2=2 So id ∈ {2, 3}. One thread needs input from id=2 and id=3. Similarly for h=10 (even): kh=1: ih=5 For w=21 (odd): kw=0: iw=11 kw=2: iw=10 So the thread needs input[2/3, 5, 10/11] → 2*1*2 = 4 spatial positions. Times 8 c_in = 32 values. Plus 4 or 8 weights (depending on number of valid kernel positions). If I compute one output per thread, each thread needs about 32 input values and 4-64 weights. That's fine. Actually, a very direct kernel could work! **Direct Kernel (one thread per output element, no shared memory):** ```cuda __global__ void conv_transpose3d_direct( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, float* __restrict__ output, int N, int C_in, int C_out, int D_in, int H_in, int W_in, int D_out, int H_out, int W_out, int K, int S, int P, int groups) { int total_outputs = N * C_out * D_out * H_out * W_out; int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; if (idx >= total_outputs) return; // Decompose idx into (n, c_out, d, h, w) int w = idx % W_out; idx /= W_out; int h = idx % H_out; idx /= H_out; int d = idx % D_out; idx /= D_out; int c_out = idx % C_out; idx /= C_out; int n = idx; int c_per_group = C_in / groups; int g = c_out / c_per_group; int c_out_local = c_out % c_per_group; float sum = 0.0f; // Precompute valid kernel offsets based on parity int d_parity = d & 1; int h_parity = h & 1; int w_parity = w & 1; for (int c_in = 0; c_in < c_per_group; ++c_in) { int actual_c_in = g * c_per_group + c_in; for (int kd = 0; kd < K; ++kd) { if ((d + 1 - kd) % 2 != 0) continue; // or: (d & 1) == (kd & 1)? Let's see: d even, kd must be 1 (odd). d odd, kd must be 0 or 2 (even). So d%2 != kd%2. int id = (d + 1 - kd) / 2; if (id < 0 || id >= D_in) continue; for (int kh = 0; kh < K; ++kh) { if ((h + 1 - kh) % 2 != 0) continue; int ih = (h + 1 - kh) / 2; if (ih < 0 || ih >= H_in) continue; for (int kw = 0; kw < K; ++kw) { if ((w + 1 - kw) % 2 != 0) continue; int iw = (w + 1 - kw) / 2; if (iw < 0 || iw >= W_in) continue; float w_val = weight[((c_out * c_per_group + c_in) * K + kd) * K * K + kh * K + kw]; // Wait, weight layout. This direct kernel avoids shared memory entirely. This is a broadcast pattern! Very cache-friendly. - Input reads: threads in a warp computing adjacent (d,h,w) with same c_in read nearby input. For interior points where no boundaries are hit, the `id < 0 || id >= D_in` conditions are always false and might be optimized out if the compiler can prove it. Actually, for a first shot, let me write a direct kernel without shared memory and see how it does. ```python conv_transpose3d_cuda = """ #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> __global__ void conv_transpose3d_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, float* __restrict__ output, int N, int C_in, int C_out, int D_in, int H_in, int W_in, int D_out, int H_out, int W_out, int K, int S, int P, int groups) { int total_outputs = N * C_out * D_out * H_out * W_out; int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; if (idx >= total_outputs) return; int w = idx % W_out; idx /= W_out; int h = idx % H_out; idx /= H_out; int d = idx % D_out; idx /= D_out; int c_out = idx % C_out; idx /= C_out; int n = idx; int c_per_group = C_in / groups; int g = c_out / c_per_group; int c_out_local = c_out % c_per_group; float sum = 0.0f; // PyTorch ConvTranspose3d weight: (C_in, C_out/groups, K, K, K) for (int c_in = 0; c_in < c_per_group; ++c_in) { int actual_c_in = g * c_per_group + c_in; int w_base = ((actual_c_in * c_per_group + c_out_local) * K) * K * K; #pragma unroll for (int kd = 0; kd < 3; ++kd) { if ((d & 1) == (kd & 1)) continue; int id = (d + 1 - kd) >> 1; if (id < 0 || id >= D_in) continue; #pragma unroll for (int kh = 0; kh < 3; ++kh) { if ((h & 1) == (kh & 1)) continue; int ih = (h + 1 - kh) >> 1; if (ih < 0 || ih >= H_in) continue; #pragma unroll for (int kw = 0; kw < 3; ++kw) { if ((w & 1) == (kw & 1)) continue; int iw = (w + 1 - kw) >> 1; if (iw < 0 || iw >= W_in) continue; float w_val = weight[w_base + (kd * K + kh) * K + kw]; float i_val = input[(((n * C_in + actual_c_in) * D_in + id) * H_in + ih) * W_in + iw]; sum += w_val * i_val; } } } } output[(((n * C_out + c_out) * D_out + d) * H_out + h) * W_out + w] = sum; } torch::Tensor conv_transpose3d_forward( torch::Tensor input, torch::Tensor weight, int stride, int padding, int groups) { int N = input.size(0); int C_in = input.size(1); int D_in = input.size(2); int H_in = input.size(3); int W_in = input.size(4); int C_out = weight.size(1) * groups; int K = weight.size(2); int D_out = (D_in - 1) * stride - 2 * padding + K; int H_out = (H_in - 1) * stride - 2 * padding + K; int W_out = (W_in - 1) * stride - 2 * padding + K; auto output = torch::zeros({N, C_out, D_out, H_out, W_out}, input.options()); int total_outputs = N * C_out * D_out * H_out * W_out; const int threads = 256; const int blocks = (total_outputs + threads - 1) / threads; conv_transpose3d_kernel<<<blocks, threads>>>( input.data_ptr<float>(), weight.data_ptr<float>(), output.data_ptr<float>(), N, C_in, C_out, D_in, H_in, W_in, D_out, H_out, W_out, K, stride, padding, groups); return output; } """ ``` Wait, I need to be careful about the weight indexing. To beat cuDNN, our custom kernel needs to be really good. **Advanced approach: Use shared memory for spatial tiles and vectorized loads** Since the kernel is small (K=3) and stride is 2, the overlapping structure means spatial tiling can reuse input very effectively. Let me design a kernel where: - One block computes a tile of output space for ALL c_out channels for one batch element. - But ALL c_out = 32 channels, which is a lot. - Or: one block computes a tile for one (n, c_out). Given that there's significant work per block, let's try: Block size: 256 threads arranged as (32 c_out_coarse, 8 spatial). Or just 256 threads handling 1 c_out over 256 spatial positions, or 32 c_out over 8 spatial positions. Actually, given that input reuse is spatial (same c_in), it's better to compute multiple c_out over the same spatial tile simultaneously. Let's try one block computes: - 8 c_out values (coarse) - 8 spatial positions (threads parallelize over both) Actually, with 256 threads: - 32 threads for c_out (but only 32 total c_out, so 1 thread per c_out within the block) - 8 threads for some spatial dimension No, let me think of it differently. If block computes output tile: (TD=4, TH=4, TW=8) = 128 spatial positions, and all 32 c_out channels. Total outputs = 128 * 32 = 4096. With 256 threads, each thread computes 16 outputs. For example, thread idx: compute 2 c_out × 8 spatial positions. For this to work with shared memory: - Load input tile into shared memory: relevant input for the spatial tile. - For TD=4, TH=4, TW=8: input tile ≈ (3, 5, 9) spatial × 32 c_in. - 3*5*9*32 = 4320 floats = 17KB. - Load weight into shared memory: 32 c_out × 32 c_in × 27 = 27,648 floats = 110KB. That's too big for shared memory. So we can't load all weights. We load input once, then stream weights. Actually, stream c_out slices: for each c_out (or small group of c_out), load weights from global memory and compute. Or: load input into shared memory, then for each c_out and c_in, compute: ``` for c_in in 0..31: load input[c_in] tile into smem (already there) for c_out in 0..31: load weight[c_out, c_in, :, :, :] into registers (27 floats = 108 bytes per thread? No, 27 floats for all threads if broadcast) compute for all spatial positions. ``` Wait, weight for one (c_out, c_in) pair is 27 floats. If we add it to shared memory or have a warp load it, all threads can access it. Actually, for each (c_in, c_out) pair, we only need 27 floats. The total "work" is loading 27 floats from global memory and doing ~3.35 spatial FMAs per output position. If a block handles 4096 outputs and we iterate over 32*32=1024 (c_in, c_out) pairs: For each (c_in, c_out) pair, all threads in the block access the same 27 weight floats. So we load 27 floats into shared memory once, then broadcast to all threads. Total weight traffic: 1024 * 27 * 4 bytes = 110KB loaded into shared memory over the block execution. That's not much. Input traffic: 17KB loaded once into shared memory. Very efficient. Output: 4096 * 4 bytes = 16KB written once per block. This seems very promising! **Detailed block design:** Block size: 256 threads Grid: For each n (4 values) and spatial tile. Spatial tile size: let's say TD=4, TH=8, TW=16 = 512 spatial positions. All c_out = 32. Total outputs = 512 * 32 = 16,384. Per thread: 16,384 / 256 = 64 outputs. That's a lot of register pressure if each thread accumulates 64 outputs. Alternative: have a block handle (TD=4, TH=4, TW=8) = 128 spatial × 8 c_out = 1024 outputs. Wait, but then we'd need to iterate over all 32 c_out in 4 passes. Or: a block handles 128 spatial × 32 c_out = 4096 outputs. 4096 / 256 = 16 outputs per thread. That's manageable: 16 floats in registers. Actually, each thread computes 16 outputs = 16 accumulators = 64 bytes. That's fine. Let's refine: block handles output tile of (TD=4, TH=4, TW=8) with ALL 32 c_out channels. Wait, 4*4*8 = 128 spatial. 128 * 32 = 4096 outputs. With 256 threads: each thread computes 16 output values. But a thread computing 16 values for different c_out needs access to different weights. Actually, transposed conv with K=3, S=2, P=1 is equivalent to: 1. Spatial upsampling (nearest neighbor or zero-insertion) 2. Conv3d with K=3, S=1, P=1 And the upsampled tensor is 8x larger in elements but only 1/8 non-zero. What if we implement the effective conv using matrix multiplication on the non-zero elements? For each output position (d, h, w), it receives contributions from at most 8 input positions. The pattern is very regular. Actually, for odd output positions in all three dimensions (d%2=1, h%2=1, w%2=1), we need 8 input values arranged in a 2×2×2 cube of the compressed input. For (d=2*dd+1, h=2*hh+1, w=2*ww+1): output uses: - input(dd, hh, ww) with (kd=2,kh=2,kw=2) - input(dd+1, hh, ww) with (kd=0,kh=2,kw=2) - input(dd, hh+1, ww) with (kd=2,kh=0,kw=2) - input(dd+1, hh+1, ww) with (kd=0,kh=0,kw=2) - input(dd, hh, ww+1) with (kd=2,kh=2,kw=0) - input(dd+1, hh, ww+1) with (kd=0,kh=2,kw=0) - input(dd, hh+1, ww+1) with (kd=2,kh=0,kw=0) - input(dd+1, hh+1, ww+1) with (kd=0,kh=0,kw=0) This is a standard 2×2×2 kernel conv on the compressed input! Similarly, for even positions, it's various simpler convs. This means each parity class corresponds to a standard dense conv3d on a downsampled version of the output grid! - Phase (0,0,0): downsampled grid size 32×64×128, conv with a single kernel position (1,1,1). - Phase (0,0,1): downsampled grid 32×64×127, conv with kernel {(_,_,0), (_,_,2)} = effectively a 1D conv in W. - Phase (1,1,1): downsampled grid 31×63×127, conv with 2×2×2 kernel. And we can compute each phase separately using standard dense convolutions! The weights for each phase are just slices of the original kernel. Original kernel elements indexed by (kd, kh, kw) where kd∈{0,1,2}, etc. Phase (0,0,0): only (1,1,1). Phase (0,0,1): (1,1,0) and (1,1,2). Phase (0,1,0): (1,0,1) and (1,2,1). ... Phase (1,1,1): all 8 corner elements (kd∈{0,2}, kh∈{0,2}, kw∈{0,2}). And for each phase, the operation is: for each (c_in, c_out), apply a small conv on an extracted input grid. This is a brilliant insight because it means we can potentially use highly optimized standard operations! Specifically: 1. Extract 8 phase-specific input grids by strided slicing. 2. For each phase, compute a standard conv3d with the appropriate small kernel. 3. Add all phase outputs together into the final output (with appropriate strided insertion). Wait, but the phase-specific input grids are just downsampled versions. And the conv kernels are tiny (1×1×1 up to 2×2×2). Can we use PyTorch's conv3d for each phase? That would involve multiple PyTorch ops, each with its own kernel launch overhead. For 8 phases, that's 8 separate conv3d calls, plus extractions. Probably slower than one cuDNN call. But we can implement each phase as a custom small kernel! Actually, for phase (0,0,0), the computation is: For each (n, c_out, dd, hh, ww): sum over c_in: weight[c_out, c_in, 1, 1, 1] * input[n, c_in, dd, hh, ww] This is just a 1×1×1 conv, which is a matrix multiplication: (N*D*H*W) × C_in × C_out. We can use a simple kernel or even just reshape + matmul. Similarly for phase (1,1,1): output[2*dd+1, 2*hh+1, 2*ww+1] = conv3d(input[dd:dd+2, hh:hh+2, ww:ww+2], kernel[0:2 step 2, 0:2 step 2, 0:2 step 2]). But implementing 8 phases with separate kernels and then combining them is complex. **Simpler phase kernel:** Actually, what if we process all phases in one kernel but with the phase-decomposed logic? For each output position, we know its phase. We compute the phase-specific small conv. Since phases are interleaved in output memory, writing them efficiently is tricky. Let me go back to the block-tiling approach. I think that's most promising. **Simplified complete kernel with shared memory:** I'll write a kernel with the following structure: - Block size: 256 threads - Block grid: (N, C_out, num_spatial_blocks) - Each block computes a tile of spatial output for one (n, c_out). - Uses shared memory for input tile. Actually, to reduce shared memory pressure and increase flexibility, let's have each block compute a tile for one (n, c_out) but with a larger spatial tile to amortize the input loading. Let's fix tile sizes: TW=16, TH=4, TD=1. Why TD=1? Because D=63 is relatively small compared to H and W, and contiguous memory access along W is best. With TD=1, TH=4, TW=16: 64 spatial outputs per block. Input tile: IH=3, IW=9, ID=1 or 2. For TD=1: If d0 even: id_min = d0/2, id_max = d0/2 (since d1=d0+1, only one value). ID=1. If d0 odd: id_min = (d0-1)/2, id_max = (d0+1)/2. ID=2. So ID is at most 2. Input tile size: 2 * 3 * 9 * C_in = 54 * C_in. For C_in=32: 1728 floats = 6.9KB. For C_in=64: 3456 floats = 13.8KB. For C_in=128: 6912 floats = 27.6KB. Weights: c_per_group * K^3 = up to about 32*27=864 floats = 3.4KB. Total shared memory: ~31KB for C_in=128. Still OK. With 64 outputs per block and 256 threads: that's 4 threads per output! We can have threads compute the same output in parallel over c_in or kernel positions. Wait, if 4 threads help compute 1 output, they need to reduce. We can do a warp-level reduction. Actually, 256 threads for 64 outputs = 4 threads per output. These 4 threads can be responsible for: - Thread 0: c_in = 0..7 - Thread 1: c_in = 8..15 - Thread 2: c_in = 16..23 - Thread 3: c_in = 24..31 Then they reduce via shared memory or warp shuffle. This parallelizes over c_in! Since c_in dimension has 32 elements, splitting across 4 threads is nice. And each thread then iterates over only 8 c_in values instead of 32. For each c_in, a thread loops over valid kd,kh,kw (1 to 8 combinations). For each combination, it does 1 multiply-add. For thread j handling c_in from j*8 to j*8+7: For each c_in: For s in its assigned spatial positions (all 64 if we organize differently, or some subset). Wait, with 4 threads per output and 64 outputs, each thread handles 1/4 of c_in for all 64 outputs? That would mean each thread computes partial sums for 64 outputs, then reduces across the 4 threads. Memory for 64 partial sums per thread: 64 * 4 bytes = 256 bytes in registers. Fine. After all c_in are processed, do a block-level reduction or warp shuffle to sum the 4 partial sums for each output. This is a common pattern and can be very efficient! **Refined design for TD=1, TH=4, TW=16:** Block: 256 threads. Outputs computed: 64 spatial positions × 1 c_out. Each output is computed by a group of 4 threads. Thread grouping: - For output index s (0..63): threads [s*4, s*4+1, s*4+2, s*4+3] compute it. Wait, but then threads s*4 all access the same spatial position. And we parallelize c_in across them. Layout: - Thread idx = 0..255 - s = threadIdx.x / 4 (0..63) - c_in_sub = threadIdx.x % 4 (0..3) - c_in_start = c_in_sub * (C_in / 4) But C_in might not be divisible by 4 if groups is weird. In our case, C_in=32, c_per_group=32 (groups=1) or 8 (groups=4). If c_per_group=8 and we have 4 threads: each thread handles 2 c_in. Or if c_per_group=32, each handles 8. Actually, the reduction is over the C_in dimension, so C_in = c_per_group. Num c_in subgroups = 4. For each s: d = d0 + s / (TH*TW) ? Actually for TD=1, s maps to (h, w): s = h_local * TW + w_local. h = h0 + h_local. w = w0 + w_local. Each thread computes: partial_sum = 0; for c_in = c_in_start to c_in_start + c_in_count - 1: for valid kd,kh,kw: partial_sum += weight[c_out_local, c_in, kd, kh, kw] * input[n, g*c_per_group+c_in, id, ih, iw] After all threads finish, we need to sum the 4 partial sums. Since threads for the same s are contiguous (s*4 to s*4+3), they are in the same warp if s*4/32 is the same warp. For s=0: threads 0,1,2,3 (warp 0). For s=7: threads 28,29,30,31 (warp 0). For s=8: threads 32,33,34,35 (warp 1). So within a warp, every 8 consecutive s values (32 threads) are fully contained. When we reduce across the 4 threads for each s, we can use warp shuffles! For each s handled by a warp, the 4 threads are threads (s%8)*4 to (s%8)*4+3 within the warp. Actually, in warp 0 (threads 0..31): s=0: threads 0,1,2,3 s=1: threads 4,5,6,7 ... s=7: threads 28,29,30,31 So for each s in a warp, the 4 threads are at lane (s%8)*4 + offset. Reduction: the 4 threads can shuffle their values. Actually, a simpler approach: after computing partial sums in registers, store them to shared memory: s_partial[s] = sum of 4 threads. Or better: have each thread write to smem, then read back and sum. Or even simpler: use `__shfl_down_sync` within the quad. For s handled by threads t_base, t_base+1, t_base+2, t_base+3: ```cuda float val = partial_sum; val += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, val, 1); // But we need specific lanes ``` Actually, lanes for s=0: 0,1,2,3. `shfl_down` from lane 3 to 2 adds lane 3 to lane 2. Then lane 2 to lane 1 adds lanes 2+3 to lane 1. Then lane 1 to lane 0 adds all. ```cuda val += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, val, 1); // lane 0 gets lane 1 val += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, val, 2); // lane 0 gets lane 2 (original value from lane 2, not updated) ``` Wait, after lane 0 adds lane 1, lane 0 has val0+val1. Then `__shfl_down_sync(val, 2)` from lane 2: lane 2 still has val2 (since lane 2 got val3 in the first shuffle, but only if it did... wait no, lane 2 got val3 if we did shfl_down with 1 from lane 3 to lane 2?) Actually, `__shfl_down_sync(mask, val, offset)` returns the value from thread lane + offset. For lanes 0,1,2,3 with values v0,v1,v2,v3: After `val += __shfl_down_sync(0xF, val, 1)`: - lane 0: v0 + v1 - lane 1: v1 + v2 - lane 2: v2 + v3 - lane 3: v3 + 0 (or undefined) After `val += __shfl_down_sync(0xF, val, 2)`: - lane 0: (v0+v1) + (v2+v3) - lane 1: (v1+v2) + v3 - etc. So lane 0 gets the full sum. Perfect! But we need to mask only the 4 threads, not the full warp, to avoid cross-s contamination. Actually, if threads 4,5,6,7 are running simultaneously in the same warp and we use mask 0xF, then for lane 0 (thread 0), shfl_down with 1 gets from lane 1 (thread 1), shfl_down with 2 gets from lane 2 (thread 2). These are within the same s=0 group. For lane 4 (thread 4, s=1), shfl_down with 1 gets from lane 5 (thread 5, s=1). Also within group. The mask 0xFFFFFFFF for the full warp is fine because each s-group is isolated in its own 4-lane segment. But wait, lane 3 shfl_down 1 gets from lane 4 (different group!). That's bad. So we need to either: 1. Use segment masks or 2. Do the shuffle within groups by ensuring offset doesn't cross group boundaries. With 4 groups per warp: s=0 uses lanes 0-3, s=1 uses 4-7, etc. `__shfl_down_sync` with offset 1: lane 3 gets from lane 4 (BAD). To fix, we can do: ```cuda unsigned int mask = __ballot_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, true); // all active // Or better, just use shared memory. ``` Shared memory approach: ```cuda __shared__ float s_reduce[64]; // 64 outputs s_reduce[s] = partial_sum; __syncthreads(); if (c_in_sub == 0) { float total = s_reduce[s] + s_reduce[s+1] + s_reduce[s+2] + s_reduce[s+3]; // Wait, indexing: s = threadIdx.x / 4, c_in_sub = threadIdx.x % 4 // So s_reduce stores partial sums at index s for each c_in_sub... no, s is the output and c_in_sub is the thread offset. // We'd need s_reduce[c_in_sub * 64 + s]. } ``` Actually: ```cuda __shared__ float s_partials[4][64]; // [c_in_sub][s] s_partials[c_in_sub][s] = partial_sum; __syncthreads(); if (c_in_sub == 0) { float total = s_partials[0][s] + s_partials[1][s] + s_partials[2][s] + s_partials[3][s]; output[...] = total; } ``` This is clean. s_partials size: 4 * 64 = 256 floats = 1KB. Plus shared memory for input and weights. Total shared: ~10KB (input) + ~4KB (weights) + 1KB (partials) = ~15KB. This design is looking really good. Let me finalize the kernel structure. But on H100, it should automatically detect or at least compile for the current GPU. Let me draft the kernel. ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline cuda_source = """ #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #define TILE_D 1 #define TILE_H 4 #define TILE_W 16 #define THREADS 256 __global__ void conv_transpose3d_tiled_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, float* __restrict__ output, int N, int C_in, int C_out, int D_in, int H_in, int W_in, int D_out, int H_out, int W_out, int K, int S, int P, int groups) { int tid = threadIdx.x; int n = blockIdx.x; int c_out = blockIdx.y; int spatial_block = blockIdx.z; int c_per_group = C_in / groups; int g = c_out / c_per_group; int c_out_local = c_out % c_per_group; int tile_spatial = TILE_D * TILE_H * TILE_W; int spatial_size = D_out * H_out * W_out; int total_spatial_blocks = (spatial_size + tile_spatial - 1) / tile_spatial; int tile_start = spatial_block * tile_spatial; int tile_end = min(tile_start + tile_spatial, spatial_size); int tile_count = tile_end - tile_start; if (tile_count <= 0) return; // Output spatial mapping // For linear idx -> (d, h, w) // d = idx / (H_out * W_out) // h = (idx % (H_out * W_out)) / W_out // w = idx % W_out // Compute input tile bounds for this output tile // Output positions in this tile: tile_start .. tile_end-1 int d_start = tile_start / (H_out * W_out); int h_start = (tile_start % (H_out * W_out)) / W_out; int w_start = tile_start % W_out; int d_end = (tile_end - 1) / (H_out * W_out) + 1; int h_end = ((tile_end - 1) % (H_out * W_out)) / W_out + 1; int w_end = (tile_end - 1) % W_out + 1; // Heuristic input bounds (safe over-estimate) // id_min = max(0, floor((d_start + P - (K-1)) / S)) // id_max = min(D_in - 1, floor((d_end - 1 + P) / S)) int id_start = (d_start + P - (K - 1)); id_start = id_start / S - ((id_start < 0 && id_start % S != 0) ? 1 : 0); // floor division for negative int id_end = (d_end - 1 + P) / S + 1; int ih_start = (h_start + P - (K - 1)); ih_start = ih_start / S - ((ih_start < 0 && ih_start % S != 0) ? 1 : 0); int ih_end = (h_end - 1 + P) / S + 1; int iw_start = (w_start + P - (K - 1)); iw_start = iw_start / S - ((iw_start < 0 && iw_start % S != 0) ? 1 : 0); int iw_end = (w_end - 1 + P) / S + 1; id_start = max(0, id_start); id_end = min(D_in, id_end); ih_start = max(0, ih_start); ih_end = min(H_in, ih_end); iw_start = max(0, iw_start); iw_end = min(W_in, iw_end); int ID = id_end - id_start; int IH = ih_end - ih_start; int IW = iw_end - iw_start; int input_tile_size = ID * IH * IW; // Shared memory sizes // s_input[c_per_group][ID][IH][IW] // s_partials[4][tile_spatial] where 4 = c_in splits // s_weight can be read directly from global or cached in smem extern __shared__ char smem[]; float* s_input = (float*)smem; int s_input_size = c_per_group * input_tile_size; float* s_partials = (float*)(smem + s_input_size * sizeof(float)); // Load input tile into shared memory // Total: c_per_group * ID * IH * IW int total_input_loads = c_per_group * input_tile_size; for (int idx = tid; idx < total_input_loads; idx += THREADS) { int iw = idx % IW; int tmp = idx / IW; int ih = tmp % IH; tmp = tmp / IH; int id = tmp % ID; int c_in = tmp / ID; int actual_c_in = g * c_per_group + c_in; int global_idx = (((n * C_in + actual_c_in) * D_in + (id_start + id)) * H_in + (ih_start + ih)) * W_in + (iw_start + iw); s_input[c_in * input_tile_size + id * IH * IW + ih * IW + iw] = input[global_idx]; } __syncthreads(); // Each thread handles: // s = tid / 4 (output spatial index within tile) // c_in_sub = tid % 4 (which quarter of c_in) int s = tid >> 2; // / 4 int c_in_sub = tid & 3; // % 4 // Number of c_in per sub-thread int c_in_per_thread = (c_per_group + 3) >> 2; // ceil(c_per_group / 4) int c_in_begin = c_in_sub * c_in_per_thread; int c_in_end = min(c_in_begin + c_in_per_thread, c_per_group); float partial = 0.0f; // Weight base index for PyTorch ConvTranspose3d: (C_in, C_out/groups, K, K, K) int w_base_outer = c_out_local * K * K * K; if (s < tile_count) { int out_linear = tile_start + s; int w = out_linear % W_out; int tmp = out_linear / W_out; int h = tmp % H_out; int d = tmp / H_out; for (int c_in = c_in_begin; c_in < c_in_end; ++c_in) { int actual_c_in = g * c_per_group + c_in; int w_base = ((actual_c_in * c_per_group) + c_out_local) * K * K * K; for (int kd = 0; kd < K; ++kd) { int num = d + P - kd; if (num % S != 0) continue; int id = num / S; if (id < id_start || id >= id_end) continue; int sid = id - id_start; for (int kh = 0; kh < K; ++kh) { int num_h = h + P - kh; if (num_h % S != 0) continue; int ih = num_h / S; if (ih < ih_start || ih >= ih_end) continue; int sih = ih - ih_start; for (int kw = 0; kw < K; ++kw) { int num_w = w + P - kw; if (num_w % S != 0) continue; int iw = num_w / S; if (iw < iw_start || iw >= iw_end) continue; int siw = iw - iw_start; float w_val = weight[w_base + kd * K * K + kh * K + kw]; float i_val = s_input[c_in * input_tile_size + sid * IH * IW + sih * IW + siw]; partial += w_val * i_val; } } } } } // Store partial to shared memory and reduce s_partials[c_in_sub * tile_spatial + s] = partial; __syncthreads(); if (s < tile_count && c_in_sub == 0) { float total = s_partials[0 * tile_spatial + s] + s_partials[1 * tile_spatial + s] + s_partials[2 * tile_spatial + s] + s_partials[3 * tile_spatial + s]; int out_linear = tile_start + s; int w = out_linear % W_out; int tmp = out_linear / W_out; int h = tmp % H_out; int d = tmp / H_out; output[(((n * C_out + c_out) * D_out + d) * H_out + h) * W_out + w] = total; } } torch::Tensor conv_transpose3d_cuda_forward( torch::Tensor input, torch::Tensor weight, c10::optional<torch::Tensor> bias, int stride, int padding, int groups) { int N = input.size(0); int C_in = input.size(1); int D_in = input.size(2); int H_in = input.size(3); int W_in = input.size(4); int C_out_per_group = weight.size(1); int C_out = C_out_per_group * groups; int K = weight.size(2); int D_out = (D_in - 1) * stride - 2 * padding + K; int H_out = (H_in - 1) * stride - 2 * padding + K; int W_out = (W_in - 1) * stride - 2 * padding + K; auto output = torch::zeros({N, C_out, D_out, H_out, W_out}, input.options()); int spatial_size = D_out * H_out * W_out; int tile_spatial = TILE_D * TILE_H * TILE_W; int num_spatial_blocks = (spatial_size + tile_spatial - 1) / tile_spatial; dim3 grid(N, C_out, num_spatial_blocks); dim3 block(THREADS); size_t smem_max_input = C_in * 64 * sizeof(float); // heuristic size_t smem_partials = 4 * tile_spatial * sizeof(float); size_t smem_size = smem_max_input + smem_partials; conv_transpose3d_tiled_kernel<<<grid, block, smem_size>>>( input.data_ptr<float>(), weight.data_ptr<float>(), output.data_ptr<float>(), N, C_in, C_out, D_in, H_in, W_in, D_out, H_out, W_out, K, stride, padding, groups); if (bias.has_value()) { output += bias.value().view({1, C_out, 1, 1, 1}); } return output; } """ conv_transpose3d_cuda = load_inline( name="conv_transpose3d_cuda", cpp_sources="torch::Tensor conv_transpose3d_cuda_forward(torch::Tensor input, torch::Tensor weight, c10::optional<torch::Tensor> bias, int stride, int padding, int groups);", cuda_sources=cuda_source, functions=["conv_transpose3d_cuda_forward"], extra_cuda_cflags=["-O3", "--use_fast_math"], ) ``` Wait, there's a problem with the weight layout. Let me verify PyTorch's ConvTranspose3d weight shape again. For ConvTranspose3d, `weight.shape` = (in_channels, out_channels // groups, *kernel_size). But in our kernel, when we access `weight[((actual_c_in * c_per_group) + c_out_local) * K*K*K + ...]`, we are assuming the flattened layout is contiguous with `actual_c_in * (c_per_group * K^3) + c_out_local * K^3 + kd*K*K + kh*K + kw`. This matches `weight[actual_c_in, c_out_local, kd, kh, kw]` in row-major order. But wait, there's a subtlety: when `groups > 1`, does PyTorch store the weight as separate contiguous blocks per group, or as a single contiguous tensor that can be indexed as above? Actually, `nn.ConvTranspose3d` weight is always a single contiguous tensor of shape `(in_channels, out_channels//groups, *kernel_size)`. For groups > 1, actual_c_in from 0 to in_channels-1 and c_out_local from 0 to out_channels//groups - 1. So the formula `actual_c_in * (c_per_group * K^3) + c_out_local * K^3 + ...` works globally, not just per group. Wait, but does this capture grouped semantics correctly? For group 0: actual_c_in = 0..7, c_out_local = 0..7 (assuming c_per_group=8). For group 1: actual_c_in = 8..15, c_out_local = 0..7. The weight for group 1 is at offset: actual_c_in=8, c_out_local=0. But since the weight has shape (C_in, C_out/groups, K, K, K), the elements for actual_c_in=8..15 and c_out_local=0..7 are indeed stored contiguously after group 0. Actually, no! For ConvTranspose3d, every input channel in one group connects to every output channel in the SAME group. But the weight tensor stores ALL groups stacked along the in_channels dimension. So actual_c_in=0..7 connect to c_out_local=0..7 (group 0's outputs), and actual_c_in=8..15 connect to c_out_local=0..7 (group 1's outputs). But wait, c_out_local is local to the group, so group 1's output channels are also labeled c_out_local=0..7 in the weight tensor. However, for a given output channel c_out, its full index is c_out. The group is g = c_out / c_per_group. The local index is c_out_local = c_out % c_per_group. And this output channel only connects to input channels in the same group: actual_c_in = g * c_per_group + c_in_local. So in the weight tensor, the slice for output c_out is at `weight[:, c_out_local, ...]`. But only certain actual_c_in indices (those in group g) are used. So when we load `weight[actual_c_in * (c_per_group * K^3) + c_out_local * K^3 + ...]`, for actual_c_in in group g and c_out_local fixed, we're reading a slice that conceptually belongs to group g. This is correct because the weight tensor just stores all these slices densely. Wait, but do they store zeros for cross-group connections? No, the weight tensor simply defines the full linear map. For grouped conv transpose, the weight tensor has shape (in_channels, out_channels//groups, ...), but cross-group weights are conceptually "not used" by the grouped operation. Are they actually present in the tensor? Yes! The weight tensor contains in_channels * (out_channels/groups) * K^3 elements. For groups=4, C_in=32, C_out=32: 32 * 8 * 27 = 6912 floats. This includes ALL the intra-group weights. There are no cross-group weights stored separately. The grouping is enforced by only using a subset of connections. More precisely: for grouped conv, weight[i, j] connects input channel i to output channel j within constraints. For group g, input channels [g*c_per_group, (g+1)*c_per_group) connect to output channels [g*c_per_group, (g+1)*c_per_group). In the weight tensor, output channel g*c_per_group + j corresponds to index j in the second dimension. So weight slice for output c_out is at `weight[:, c_out_local, ...]`, but only the actual_c_in values in the same group are actually used by the convolution operation. And our indexing formula does exactly that: for output c_out (with c_out_local), we iterate c_in over the group only (c_in=0..c_per_group-1, actual_c_in = g*c_per_group + c_in). Actually, we can have the grid be just `num_blocks` 1D, and each block computes a specific assignment based on blockIdx.x. ```cpp int block_id = blockIdx.x; int n = block_id / (C_out * num_spatial_blocks); int tmp = block_id % (C_out * num_spatial_blocks); int c_out = tmp / num_spatial_blocks; int spatial_block_id = tmp % num_spatial_blocks; ``` Then spatial_block_id maps to a 3D tile origin: ```cpp int num_w_tiles = (W_out + TW - 1) / TW; int num_h_tiles = (H_out + TH - 1) / TH; int num_d_tiles = (D_out + TD - 1) / TD; int tile_w = spatial_block_id % num_w_tiles; int tmp2 = spatial_block_id / num_w_tiles; int tile_h = tmp2 % num_h_tiles; int tile_d = tmp2 / num_h_tiles; int w0 = tile_w * TW; int h0 = tile_h * TH; int d0 = tile_d * TD; ``` And the tile covers [d0, min(d0+TD, D_out)), etc. No wrap-around! Each tile is a rectangular box. Grid size = N * C_out * num_d_tiles * num_h_tiles * num_w_tiles. For N=4, C_out=32, D_tiles=63/1=63, H_tiles=127/4=32, W_tiles=255/16=16. Now I need to understand the operation: - nn.ConvTranspose3d is a transposed (fractionally strided) 3D convolution - It increases spatial dimensions based on stride - With stride=2, kernel_size=3, padding=1: - output_size = (input_size - 1) * stride - 2 * padding + kernel_size - For depth: (32 - 1) * 2 - 2 * 1 + 3 = 31 * 2 - 2 + 3 = 62 + 1 = 63 - For height: (64 - 1) * 2 + 1 = 127 - For width: (128 - 1) * 2 + 1 = 255 Transposed convolution can be thought of as convolving with a dilated/padded version of the input. A common approach is to use the fact that transposed convolution is equivalent to: 1. Upsample the input (with zeros between elements) 2. Apply a regular convolution For performance on H100, I should consider: - Using shared memory tiles - Coalesced memory access patterns - Avoiding atomics if possible - Computing directly without materializing the upsampled input Actually, for transposed convolution, there's a common optimization where each input element contributes to a k×k×k neighborhood in the output. This is like scatter-gather. Let me think about a more efficient approach. For stride=2, we can think of it as: - Each input pixel generates a kernel_size^3 region in output - With padding=1, the "effective" output region may be different Actually, I should verify the expected output dimensions first. Let me think about the math: ConvTranspose3d with: - stride=2 - padding=1 - kernel_size=3 - groups=4 (so group conv) Output shape formula for ConvTranspose3d: D_out = (D_in - 1) * stride - 2*padding + kernel_size + output_padding With D_in=32, stride=2, padding=1, kernel_size=3, output_padding=0: D_out = (32-1)*2 - 2*1 + 3 = 62 - 2 + 3 = 63 Similarly for height: (64-1)*2 -2 +3 = 127 For width: (128-1)*2 -2 +3 = 255 So output is [batch=4, out_channels=32, D=63, H=127, W=255] Since groups=4 and channels=32, each group has 8 input channels and 8 output channels. For a transposed convolution implementation, I can think of it as: For each output position, it's computed by summing contributions from all nearby input positions where the kernel overlaps. But the more CUDA-friendly way is: For each input position, it "splatters" its value times the corresponding kernel weight to a region in the output. This is the "scatter" approach. However, scatter with atomics is inefficient. The "gather" approach is better for parallelization: For each output position, determine which input positions and kernel elements contribute to it. Let me think about gather: For output position (b, c_out, d, h, w), which input positions contribute? In transposed conv, this is equivalent to: output[b, c_out, d, h, w] = sum over all input positions and kernel elements where: d = d_in * stride + kd - padding ... wait let me get the formula right. Actually, ConvTranspose3d is equivalent to: 1. Insert (stride-1) zeros between input elements in all spatial dims 2. Do a regular convolution For stride=2, padding=1, kernel_size=3: - Insert 1 zero between each input element - The "transposed" padding for equivalent regular conv is kernel_size - padding - 1 = 3 - 1 - 1 = 1 on each side - So pad with 1 on each side - Then do regular conv with stride=1 Let me think about this differently. Let me write a simple direct kernel: ```cuda __global__ void conv_transpose3d_kernel( float* output, const float* input, const float* weight, int batch, int in_ch, int out_ch, int D, int H, int W, int k, int s, int p, int groups) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int total = batch * out_ch * D_out * H_out * W_out; if (idx >= total) return; // decode idx to b, c_out, d, h, w ... int g = c_out / (out_ch / groups); int oc = c_out % (out_ch / groups); int ic_per_group = in_ch / groups; float acc = 0.0f; for (int ic = 0; ic < ic_per_group; ic++) { int in_c = g * ic_per_group + ic; for (int kd = 0; kd < k; kd++) { if ((d + p - kd) % s != 0) continue; int di = (d + p - kd) / s; if (di < 0 || di >= D_in) continue; for (int kh = 0; kh < k; kh++) { if ((h + p - kh) % s != 0) continue; int hi = (h + p - kh) / s; if (hi < 0 || hi >= H_in) continue; for (int kw = 0; kw < k; kw++) { if ((w + p - kw) % s != 0) continue; int wi = (w + p - kw) / s; if (wi < 0 || wi >= W_in) continue; float i_val = input[...]; float w_val = weight[in_c * (out_ch/groups) * k*k*k + oc * k*k*k + kd*k*k + kh*k + kw]; acc += i_val * w_val; } } } } output[idx] = acc; } ``` This is very naive but should be correct. Let me optimize from there. Wait, I should first check what PyTorch CUDNN gives us as baseline. But I don't have a way to measure performance directly. Let me just try to write the best kernel I can. For optimization, I think the phase-based approach is promising. Let me formalize it. For each phase (pd, ph, pw) in {0,1}^3: - Output positions are those where d%2=pd, h%2=ph, w%2=pw - Valid kernel positions are: kd in Kd(pd) = {kd | (pd + p - kd) % s == 0} With p=1, s=2: (pd + 1 - kd) % 2 == 0 => kd % 2 == (pd + 1) % 2 If pd=0: kd%2==1 => kd=1 If pd=1: kd%2==0 => kd=0,2 So Kd(0)={1}, Kd(1)={0,2} Similarly for Kh, Kw. For phase (pd, ph, pw), define output sub-tensor: out_phase[b, c_out, d', h', w'] = output[b, c_out, 2d'+pd, 2h'+ph, 2w'+pw] where 2d'+pd < D_out, etc. And for each valid (kd, kh, kw), define the input offset: di = (2d' + pd + 1 - kd) / 2 For pd=0, kd=1: di = (2d' + 1 - 1)/2 = d' For pd=1, kd=0: di = (2d' + 1 + 1 - 0)/2 = d' + 1 For pd=1, kd=2: di = (2d' + 1 + 1 - 2)/2 = d' Interesting! For each phase, the input offsets are constant offsets from (d', h', w'). Let me make a table for all phases: Phase (0,0,0): kd=1,kh=1,kw=1 -> di=d', hi=h', wi=w' So out = sum_ic weight[ic, oc, 1,1,1] * input[..., d', h', w'] This is a 1x1x1 conv! Only uses weight at position (1,1,1). Phase (0,0,1): kw in {0,2} For kw=0: wi = (2w'+1+1-0)/2 = w'+1 For kw=2: wi = (2w'+1+1-2)/2 = w' out = sum_ic [weight[ic,oc,1,1,0] * input[...,d',h',w'+1] + weight[ic,oc,1,1,2] * input[...,d',h',w']] Phase (1,1,1): 8 combinations kd=0: di=d'+1, kd=2: di=d' kh=0: hi=h'+1, kh=2: hi=h' kw=0: wi=w'+1, kw=2: wi=w' out = sum_ic [ w[ic,oc,0,0,0] * in[d'+1,h'+1,w'+1] + w[ic,oc,0,0,2] * in[d'+1,h'+1,w'] + w[ic,oc,0,2,0] * in[d'+1,h',w'+1] + w[ic,oc,0,2,2] * in[d'+1,h',w'] + w[ic,oc,2,0,0] * in[d',h'+1,w'+1] + w[ic,oc,2,0,2] * in[d',h'+1,w'] + w[ic,oc,2,2,0] * in[d',h',w'+1] + w[ic,oc,2,2,2] * in[d',h',w'] ] So each phase is actually evaluating a different "sub-kernel" at a downsampled grid with different boundary conditions. This structure is really nice! For each phase, we can write a specialized kernel. Phase (0,0,0): Just a 1x1x1 pointwise conv at stride 1 on the downsampled input. Input positions: d' in [0, 31], h' in [0, 31], w' in [0, 63] (since D_out=63, so 2d'<63 => d'<32, but 2d' max is 62, so d' in [0,31]) Wait, D_out = 63, so for pd=0: 2d' < 63 => d' in [0, 31] For pd=1: 2d'+1 < 63 => 2d' < 62 => d' in [0, 30] So the output sub-tensor sizes are: - pd=0: d' in [0, 31] (32 elements) - pd=1: d' in [0, 30] (31 elements) Same for H and W: - ph=0: h' in [0, 63] (64 elements) - ph=1: h' in [0, 62] (63 elements) - pw=0: w' in [0, 127] (128 elements) - pw=1: w' in [0, 126] (127 elements) Phase (0,0,0): output size [4, 32, 32, 64, 128] = 33.5M elements, each with 8 MACs = 268M MACs Phase (1,1,1): output size [4, 32, 31, 63, 127] = 31.6M elements, each with 64 MACs = 2.02B MACs Total: roughly 7B MACs as computed before. Actually, I realize I should express each phase as a regular conv with a smaller kernel. For phase (pd, ph, pw): - Define sub-kernel weights w_sub[ic, oc, i, j, l] = w[ic, oc, kd_i, kh_j, kw_l] where kd_i, kh_j, kw_l enumerate Kd(pd), Kh(ph), Kw(pw) - Define sub-kernel sizes: |Kd(pd)| x |Kh(ph)| x |Kw(pw)|, which is 1x1x1, 1x1x2, 1x2x1, etc., or 2x2x2 - The "convolution" is: out_phase = conv(input, w_sub) with the input potentially shifted Wait, the input might need padding on some sides. For example, phase (1,1,1) with kd=0 accesses di=d'+1, so the maximum di is 31+1=32, which is at the boundary (D_in=32, so di in [0,31]). For phase (1,1,1): di in {d', d'+1}, d' in [0,30], so di in [0,31], all valid! Actually, wait. For d'=30, kd=0 gives di=31, which is valid (since D_in=32). What about other phases? I'll start with a basic version and iterate. For the basic version: ```cuda __global__ void conv_transposed3d( float* __restrict__ out, const float* __restrict__ in, const float* __restrict__ weight, int B, int G, int IC_PG, int OC_PG, int D_in, int H_in, int W_in, int D_out, int H_out, int W_out, int k, int s, int p) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int total = B * G * OC_PG * D_out * H_out * W_out; if (idx >= total) return; int tmp = idx; int w = tmp % W_out; tmp /= W_out; int h = tmp % H_out; tmp /= H_out; int d = tmp % D_out; tmp /= D_out; int oc = tmp % OC_PG; tmp /= OC_PG; int g = tmp % G; tmp /= G; int b = tmp; float acc = 0.0f; for (int ic = 0; ic < IC_PG; ic++) { int in_c = g * IC_PG + ic; int w_base = in_c * D_in * H_in * W_in; int w_oc_base = (in_c * OC_PG + oc) * k * k * k; for (int kd = 0; kd < k; kd++) { int num = d + p - kd; if (num % s != 0) continue; int di = num / s; if (di < 0 || di >= D_in) continue; for (int kh = 0; kh < k; kh++) { int num_h = h + p - kh; if (num_h % s != 0) continue; int hi = num_h / s; if (hi < 0 || hi >= H_in) continue; for (int kw = 0; kw < k; kw++) { int num_w = w + p - kw; if (num_w % s != 0) continue; int wi = num_w / s; if (wi < 0 || wi >= W_in) continue; float i_val = in[w_base + di * H_in * W_in + hi * W_in + wi]; float w_val = weight[w_oc_base + kd * k * k + kh * k + kw]; acc += i_val * w_val; } } } } int out_idx = ((b * G + g) * OC_PG + oc) * D_out * H_out * W_out + d * H_out * W_out + h * W_out + w; out[out_idx] = acc; } ``` This is simple but inefficient due to: 1. Non-coalesced memory access (weights are read differently per thread) 2. Branch divergence (mod operations and conditionals) 3. No shared memory reuse Let me estimate performance: each thread does ~27 FLOPs and ~27 memory reads. If memory bandwidth is 3.35 TB/s and we read ~27 bytes per output, then throughput is ~3.35TB / 27B * 1 element = ~124M elements/s. But we have 261M output elements, so this would take ~2 seconds! That's way too slow. Wait, that can't be right because with caching, some accesses will hit cache. But still, this naive kernel will be very slow. For comparison, PyTorch CUDNN likely does this in milliseconds, not seconds. I need a much better kernel. Let me think about this more carefully. The key insight: for a given (b, g, oc, d, h), as w varies across the full range, the set of (wi, kw) pairs that contribute are repetitive. But more importantly, if I process a block of w values together, I can reuse the loaded input values. Let me think about a warp processing w = W_start to W_start + 31. For fixed (b, g, ic, d, h, kd, kh): - Threads in the warp iterate over kw and check if (w + p - kw) % s == 0 - This means each thread accesses a different wi If s=2, p=1: For w even (w%2=0): kw=1, wi = w/2 For w odd (w%2=1): kw=0, wi=(w+1)/2 or kw=2, wi=(w-1)/2 Wait, for w odd: (w+1-kw)%2==0 w odd => w+1 even => kw even => kw=0 or 2 For kw=0: wi=(w+1)/2 For kw=2: wi=(w-1)/2 So for w=1: kw=0, wi=1 or kw=2, wi=0 For w=3: kw=0, wi=2 or kw=2, wi=1 ... Interesting! For w odd, the two valid kw values access adjacent wi values. If I have a warp with w = 0, 1, 2, 3, ..., 31: - w=0 (even): kw=1, wi=0 - w=1 (odd): kw=0, wi=1 and kw=2, wi=0 - w=2 (even): kw=1, wi=1 - w=3 (odd): kw=0, wi=2 and kw=2, wi=1 For wi=0 accessed by: w=0 (kw=1), w=1 (kw=2) For wi=1 accessed by: w=1 (kw=0), w=2 (kw=1), w=3 (kw=2) So adjacent w values share many input positions! This means if we process w in order, we have good temporal locality. Now for the kernel design, let me think about loading input into shared memory.
